Here's a @larry_levitt thread about those kinds of tradeoffs https://t.co/Mi4T98lOpI (9)
My piece doesn't talk much about the fiscal implications (didn't want to overwhelm!) but this bill requires less federal spending than "Medicare for All" -- chiefly bc the federal government wouldn't absorb as many individual health expenses. Again, for better or worse. (8)
Basically, this bill is an effort to find a middle path to universal coverage. 		It's more ambitious than modest expansions of Medicare or Medicaid, but less ambitious than "Medicare for All." 		For better or worse, depending on your perspective. (7)
In this new bill, government would get a lot more involved in regulating prices -- not just for drugs but for doctor and hospitals too.		Again, much like "Medicare for All" would do, though less aggressively and less directly. (6)
The coverage in "Medicare for America," as sponsors @RosaDeLauro &amp; @JanSchakowsky call it, would be a lot more generous than standard ACA policies, though still less generous than what the @BernieSanders bill would offer. (5)
The proposal would also preserve Medicare Advantage.		That's the private insurance option that already exists within Medicare &amp; currently enrolls roughly 1/3 of seniors. Under the new proposal, it'd be available to people of all ages, though under tighter regulation. (4)
The basic concept is to create a new, government-run plan that will absorb existing federal programs, while allowing large employers to keep offering benefits. 		Employees would then get a choice: Stay with the company plan, or enroll in the new government program. (3)
It has a lot in common with two proposals that have been in circulation for a while. 		One from @amprog https://t.co/ProbRCsQbP		And one from @JonWalkerDC https://t.co/m6rz6eGfGW (2)

Again, I don't know who is right. M4A advocates make some compelling arguments. But the history of health reform suggests the skepticism is not crazy. (9)
While others don't question the goal, but doubt it's feasible in near future -- and think quickest, surest way there is a series of smaller steps rather than an all-in-one shot that fails and potentially sets back cause. (8)
But I'd take respectful issue with last line -- "whenever Democrats question Medicare-for-all, what they're really questioning is universal coverage."		Some skeptics question whether M4A in strictest definition (I.e, Sanders) is the only worthwhile form of universal coverage. (7)
BTW the plug by @jeffspross for all-payer rate setting at the end is really important.		It's the part of health care reform that gets least attention from the public, but is absolutely essential to bringing down actual costs. (6)
Could be right, could be wrong -- and of course there are strategic questions about whether you set a lofty goal and compromise later, rather than lead with second- or third-best.		All very complicated! Which is why it's good Dems have at least 2 years to debate this. (5)
More generally, though, some M4A skeptics simply think they cannot win all of those fights -- new taxes, new spending, squeezing providers -- in the short term, especially if they are waging all of them at once. 		So they are trying to get the biggest gains they can for now. (4)
That said, a big hesitation with M4A -- at least the version associated with @SenSanders @RepJayapal and others -- is that it requires moving people out of current coverage. That scares a lot of people, though there are clear upsides too. (3)
Certainly true that, in the long run, you don't get to truly universal coverage, at an affordable price for society, without some combo of new taxes and spending, and taking on the health care industry in some way/shape/form. 		M4A has lots of political upsides, too. (2)
Fair-minded response to the M4A skepticism that's been some getting press lately (including from me).		It's from @jeffspross and worth a read. (1)  https://t.co/QC10FBQlyQ

My impression is that many M4A advocates grasp the obstacles in their path. They are playing a long game, sketching out their ideal vision. They see no point in compromising now.		What that means for politics in the next two years, I honestly do not know.
Or you could end up with a more generous version of the ACA -- maybe with fewer, clearer options that include some version of Medicare, Medicaid, or a new government plan. And maybe some combo of automatic enrollment and price regulation. Who knows.
You could also end up with a system in which the government provides basic, less generous coverage -- and private supplemental plans, subsidized for those who need them, cover the rest.		Many European systems look like this. https://t.co/41rxWOJLr3
Of course, we're talking about the M4A legislation as currently written. If Congress took it up seriously, it would probably modify the legislation and scale it back. 		You could end up with something like this: https://t.co/7KmHLzT2Ri

Private insurance has a significant role in many national health systems, including some that qualify as single-payer. But the Sanders bill, which Harris co-sponsored, would allow for only a niche supplementary market. If that.
BTW there seems to be some confusion about whether Medicare for All -- at least as its chief promoters envision it -- would really displace existing private insurance arrangements. It would.
Here's a @philipaklein thread on the politics of M4A. It sketches out, in more detail, an argument that critics are already starting to make. https://t.co/ZEuAc0SWF6
